    When a person enters a room drunk and full, the energy accumulates in the spleen and stomach, dominating the flow of fluids. If the yin energy is weak and the yang energy enters, the stomach becomes unbalanced. If the stomach is unbalanced, the essence and vitality wither, and when the essence withers, the limbs are not nourished. The effects of alcohol and food gas combine to create internal heat, leading to red urine; the energy becomes robust yet reckless. When the kidney energy is weakened and yang energy prevails alone, the hands and feet become hot and lead to heat syncope.

    There is also syncope that causes a person to feel abdominal fullness without awareness; some may regain awareness after one or two days, while others may suddenly become confused and lose consciousness, all due to chaos caused by external pathogens and rebellious yang energy, leading to insufficient motion in the shaoyin kidney meridian. When the kidney energy is minimal, the essence and blood scatter, forcing energy upwards into the chest cavity where the ancestral energy stagnates beneath the heart, and yang energy retreats downwards. The heat returns to the yin lower regions, assisting the yin and causing loss of sensation in the body.

    Moreover, the five vessels converge in the ears; if all five are blocked, it causes bodily pulses to stir while the form remains oblivious, resembling a corpse, hence it is called corpse syncope. There may be drooling like a dragging saw, with sounds in the throat representing phlegm syncope. Twitching of the hands and feet indicates wind syncope. If this occurs due to intoxication, it is called alcohol syncope; if from sudden anger, it is termed qi syncope. Bone pain and nail brittleness represent bone syncope. Sudden cramping and inability to flex or extend the toes signify arm syncope. A body that is rigid like a plank represents liver syncope. Shortness of breath and lamentation, accompanied by frantic running or climbing, correspond to yangming syncope. All these result from rebellious energy.[49p]
    Jue Men

       Dong Yuan Shi Shu [Yuan, Li Gao]

       Differentiation of Jue in Shao Han Si Ni

 Cheng WujI noted in his commentary on "On Cold Damage" that Si Ni refers to the coldness of the four limbs. Jue signifies cold hands and feet. When the cold pathogen affects the three Yang channels, the hands and feet are bound to feel hot; when it reaches the Tai Yin channel, the hands and feet will self-warm; but when it descends to the Shao Yin, the pathogen's heat intensifies, resulting in coldness of the four limbs, leading to even greater Jue. The classics state: in Shao Yin disease with Si Ni, a person may cough, experience palpitations, have difficulty urinating, suffer abdominal pain, or endure severe diarrhea. The treatment for Si Ni should use Si Ni San, which consists of Chai Hu, Zhi Shi, Shao Yao, and Gan Cao, all of which are cold substances specifically targeting the Si Ni condition, indicating that Si Ni is not solely a manifestation of empty cold. Si Ni and Jue are similar but distinct. The classics further state: various manifestations of Si Ni and Jue should not be treated with purgation, illustrating the differences between Si Ni and Jue.[50p]

    Ah! This statement! Is it not supposed to indicate something very close to reason yet very confusing in truth? I have attempted to examine it; the words of Zhongjing regarding "sijue" and "jue" are not the same: some say "sijue," some say "jue," some say "jue逆," some say "jue冷," some say "jue寒," some say "hands and feet reverse cold," some say "hands and feet jue逆," some say "hands and feet jue冷," or "hands and feet jue逆冷." Upon detailed examination, they all refer to cold. Therefore, the two words "jue逆" are often used interchangeably, and there has never been a distinction made where "逆" denotes warmth and "厥" denotes cold. However, the limbs and hands and feet should be distinguished. The addition of the character "四" (four) before "逆" refers broadly to general coldness in the hands and feet, while the addition of "手足" (hands and feet) to "jue逆," "jue冷," etc., refers specifically to hands and feet. Whenit states "not warm," it indicates cold; what difference could there be? What Zhongjing refers to as various "sijue" and "jue" should not be mistaken for being treated differently; "sijue" denotes general cold in the four limbs and "jue" refers specifically to the cold in the hands and feet, while the arms and legs above are not cold. It does not imply there is a distinction between "逆" and "厥" regarding warmth and cold. Therefore, it is stated that "jue" means hands and feet reverse cold. This explains that "逆冷" and "厥" convey the same meaning; thus, "逆" is equivalent to "厥," and "厥" to "逆." Hence, the dictionaries state: "厥" means "逆."[51p]
    Although both types of counterflow are associated with cold, there are distinctions between yin and yang. When heat reaches its extreme and leads to counterflow, yang resembles yin; when cold reaches its extreme and leads to counterflow, there is only yin without yang. When yang resembles yin, one must use cold herbs; when there is only yin without yang, one must use hot herbs. Zhongjing treated the condition of four reversals with cold herbs using Si Ni San, which addresses the case where yang resembles yin. For those who do not use the hot herbs in Si Ni Tang to treat the four reversals, how can they be sure that it is not due to a loss of original balance? Moreover, the name "Si Ni Tang" derives from the coldness in the four limbs. Now, using Si Ni Tang to treat the cold hands and feet, is it not essentially the same as counterflow? Since "Si Ni" indicates the limbs are cold, and "counterflow" refers to hands and feet being particularly cold, why then is the medicine for counterflow not named "Si Jue Tang"? Cheng proposed in the context of treating four reversals that the condition is due to heat evil, and he also stated in the "Ming Li Lun" that four reversals are not signs of mere cold deficiency.[52p]
    As for the death signs in Shaoyin disease mentioned in the two sections below, it states that the condition of Sini (four reversals) indicates severe cold. If this is the case, can it not contradict its own argument? It is understood that Sini is similar to the condition of extremities being cold or hot, and it cannot be said that Sini is solely caused by evil heat. However, if all four limbs are uniformly cold, compared to just the hands and feet being cold, there is a difference. Thus, Zhongjing said: In Shaoyin disease, those who have vomiting, diarrhea, agitation, and Sini are to die. He also said: In Shaoyin disease, if there is Sini with aversion to cold, the body curled up, the pulse not present, and no agitation but restlessness, they will die. He further stated: In Shaoyin disease, if there is vomiting, diarrhea, and coldness in the hands and feet, with restlessness and a desire to die, Wuzhuyu decoction is the main treatment. Among these three statements, two indicate death and one indicates treatability; although they manifest through various symptoms, the death cases are described with Sini, while the treatable case refers to the coldness in the extremities. This shows a distinction in severity between Sini and the coldness of the hands and feet. If all four limbs are uniformly cold, the condition is severe; whereas if only the hands and feet are cold, the condition is light. Even women and children can understand this. Cheng argues, how can the condition of extremities be worse than the reversals? If one can understand the differences between Sini and the coldness of the extremities, it is evident that Sini refers specifically to the hands and feet, while the former encompasses the arms, legs, and above. Thus, it is unnecessary to create convoluted explanations about warmth and cold, and clarity will naturally be achieved.[53p]
    Danxi Heart Method
    (Yuan Dynasty - Zhu Zhenheng)

    Reversal of Extremities

    Reversal of extremities refers to the hands and feet becoming cold due to reversed qi and blood. This condition mainly arises from qi deficiency, but can also be due to blood deficiency. In qi deficiency, the pulse is thin; in blood deficiency, the pulse is large. During heat reversal, the pulse is rapid; with external pathogens, the pulse is deep and strong; with phlegm, the pulse is wiry. For phlegm, use Atractylodes and bamboo sap; for qi deficiency, the Four Gentlemen Decoction; for blood deficiency, the Four Substances Decoction; for heat reversal, use the Major Order Decoction; for external pathogens, use the Double Relief Powder with ginger juice and wine. There are also yin reversal and yang reversal: when yin declines below, it causes heat; when yang declines below, it causes cold. Additionally, if there is numbness in the hands and feet, it indicates qi deficiency; if the hands and feet are stiff, it pertains to damp phlegm and stagnant blood; numbness in all ten fingers indicates damp phlegm and stagnant blood in the stomach.

    Reversal signifies severity, brevity, and opposition, where the hands and feet feel cold and reversed. The symptoms vary widely, and the methods for dispersing them are numerous; here, I will briefly summarize. For example, in cases of cold and heat reversal, it represents the dual reversals of yin and yang. Yang reversal occurs when heat is deep, as excessive yang leads to reversal; one must not treat this as a yin condition with heat remedies, as it could result in the spirit and essence being extinguished, leading to death. Urgently apply the Major and Minor Order Decoctions, adapting to the severity of the case.[54p]
    The term "Yin Jue" refers to a condition where the body becomes cold, the pulse is deep, the limbs are cold, the feet curl up while lying down, the lips and mouth appear blue, and there may be spontaneous diarrhea without thirst, with urination that is pale. These are the signs to observe. Treat it with warming medicines like Si Ni San and Li Zhong Wan, and promptly apply moxibustion to the lower abdomen a hundred times.

    "Shi Jue", "Fei Shi", and "Zuo Jue" are symptoms that suggest a serious condition caused by invasion of malevolent qi. It can happen suddenly and may cause coldness in the hands and feet, the skin can become bumpy, the face may turn dark, the spirit may be disturbed, and the person could speak nonsensically, tighten their jaws, or become unconscious and disoriented. These symptoms may arise after exposure to funerals, entering temples, or ascending graves. Administer Su He Wan; if there is slight recovery, follow with Qiao Qi San or Ping Wei San, known as the Qi Regulation and Stomach Peace Powder.

    "Phlegm Jue" occurs due to cold phlegm causing blockage, resulting in cold limbs. Ginger and Fu Zi Decoction is recommended, using raw Fu Zi instead of cooked.

    "Tai Jue" results from cold in the stomach. The text states that "Tai" refers to a long worm. Coldness in the stomach can lead to the expulsion of tapeworms. Use Li Zhong Tang with five fried Sichuan peppers and half a penny of betel nut, swallowing with Wu Mei Wan for effect. Tapeworms are subdued by Sichuan peppers.

    "Qi Jue" is similar to a stroke. How does one distinguish between them? In a wind stroke, the body feels warm, while with Qi Jue, the body feels cold. Use Ba Wei Shun Qi San or Qiao Qi San; if phlegm is present, administer Si Qi Decoction or Phlegm Resolving Decoction.[55p]
    Hygienic Treasure
    By Luo Tianyi (Yuan Dynasty)

    Reversal of Qi

    When the yang is reversed, the hands and feet may feel cold, though at times they may be warm; the palms and soles will surely feel warm. The pulse may be deep and hidden, but upon pressing, it is slippery. Symptoms may include a fear of heat, thirst for water, restlessness with the desire to toss and turn, difficulty sleeping, constipation, and red urine. This condition is called heat reversal, which the ancients referred to as yang reaching its extreme and then reversing. For treatment, one can use Bai Hu Decoction, Da Cheng Qi Decoction, Shuang Jie San, and Liang Ge San, carefully selecting based on the severity of symptoms.

    In contrast, for yin reversal, the limbs are cold, the body is not hot, there may be nausea, curling of the feet while lying down, or the inclination to cover oneself with clothes or blankets. There is often no thirst or diarrhea, or normal bowel movements may occur; the pulse is deep, faint, and not rapid; although it may be deep and solid, it is slow and weak upon palpation. This condition is called cold reversal. For treatment, one can use Tong Mai Si Ni Decoction, or Dang Gui Si Ni Decoction, or Bai Tong with pig gallbladder juice.[56p]
    If a patient experiences chills and fever with cold extremities, a dull complexion, sudden loss of pulse in both hands, or in one hand, this indicates the potential for a productive sweat. In this case, it is advisable to use Ma Huang Fu Zi Gan Cao Tang to assist in sweating; once sweating occurs, recovery will follow. It is also acceptable not to use medication to assist, as sweating will naturally occur.

    For various ailments with cold and clammy hands and feet, or slightly elevated body temperature, and a pulse that is deep, thin, weak, and accompanied by restlessness, treat with Si Ni Tang plus green onion, or Bai Tong with pig gallbladder soup, or Ren Shen San Bai Tang with bamboo shavings, or Wu You San; choose among these methods as suitable. For cold hands and feet with a thin and weak pulse and incoherent speech, treat with Si Ni Tang; for a cool body with cold hands and feet and a weak sound, use Si Ni Tang; if the body is cold with cold extremities and hiccups, apply Si Ni Tang plus Ren Shen; if there is vomiting and slight body heat, or cold extremities or restlessness with weak pulse, use Si Ni Tang; if there is vomiting with cold hands and feet or restlessness, treat with Si Ni Tang.

    — Medical Introduction, Ming Dynasty, Li Zhan[57p]

    Summary

    In the section on Qi Counterflow from the "Inner Canon": Counterflow is a reverse of Qi. Any shift from cold to heat, or if heat is deeply rooted and causes trembling, or if there is excessive cold leading to agitation, are all termed as counterflow, not limited to just cold hands and feet. The zong muscle corresponds to the yin vessels, with the liver and spleen meridians closely associated with the zong muscle. In cold counterflow, the yin contracts and the limbs are cold; in heat counterflow, the fluids dry up and fail to nourish the limbs, resulting in red urine and hot hands and feet. Thus, the counterflow of the six meridians is all unified under the liver and spleen. When the pathogenic factor from cold damage affects the three yang meridians, the limbs feel warm. If it’s half exterior and half interior, affecting the taiyin, the pathogen gradually penetrates inward, making the limbs warm. If it reaches the shaoyin counterflow, the pathogen enters deeply and remains embedded internally, leading to cold limbs. However, if heat leads to counterflow, it is heat counterflow through the channels, where mild cases can be treated with formulas like Si Ni San, Bai Hu Tang, or Zhu Ye Shi Gao Tang, and severe cases may require Da Chai Hu Cheng Qi Decoction. If the pathogen abruptly clogs the chest, causing restlessness and lack of appetite along with counterflow, Guati San can help expel it.

    According to "Bai Wen," heat counterflow and cold counterflow are different. Heat counterflow is when there is mild counterflow alongside fever. In contrast, cold counterflow presents without fever, with cold limbs, a deep and thin pulse, loose stools, aversion to cold, and blue-tinted nails. Additionally, if there is failure to descend, resulting in blocked blood flow with cold limbs leading to counterflow, a physician might misinterpret it as yin counterflow, leading to disastrous consequences if treated with formulas for counterflow like Si Ni. Generally, heat counterflow has a deep and slippery pulse, sweat on the forehead, warm palms, and warm fingertips, and is appropriately treated with downward actions. This is the subtle intention of Zhongjing.[58p]
    Hui Jue

    The term "Hui" refers to the roundworm, and it is also called "Jue." This condition belongs to the Jue Yin category of diseases, characterized by the extremities being cold and reversed. Patients typically have a history of consuming roundworms, or it may be due to illness and excessive hunger, where the worms ascend to the throat. If one excessively sweats, this leads to stomach coldness, allowing the worms to rise and attack the throat, causing a deficiency of stomach qi, resulting in an inability to eat even when hungry. When a person eats, if they vomit up the worms, it is because the worms detect the smell of food and emerge.

    Externally, symptoms may fluctuate between calmness and agitation, indicating that the worms may be either active or at rest. There may also be diarrhea due to cold in the internal organs, which causes the worms to move upward into the throat. Thus, when the upper part of the body is hot while the middle and lower parts are cold, symptoms of agitation, heat, and thirst may arise, accompanied by constipation. It is unwise to quickly use cooling medicines in this case. One should first use Wu Mei pills or Li Zhong Decoction to calm the roundworms, and then adjust treatment according to the symptoms. If agitation and heat persist, Xiao Chai Hu Decoction may be used. If there is significant heat, confusion, abdominal distension, constipation, or if the roundworms are restless and expelled with bowel movements, then Da Chai Hu Decoction is appropriate.[59p]
    Yin Jue

    Yin Jue refers to a condition where there is no headache, no fever, vomiting, or diarrhea before the onset of the attack, and there is a lack of thirst and a tendency to curl up; this occurs because the Yin pathogenic factor is predominant. The hands and feet are extremely cold, and this is governed by the Jue Yin, as the interaction of Yin and Yang qi does not harmonize, which is different from the gradual cooling seen in Sì Nì (Four Reverses). In cases of Tài Yīn Jue, where the hands and feet are slightly cold, the remedy is Lǐ Zhōng Tānɡ. In Shào Yīn Jue, if the shins are cold and the feet are cold, and if severe, the hands and arms are cold up to the knees; the remedies are Sì Nì Tāng or Tōnɡ Mài Sì Nì Tāng. In Jue Yīn Jue, if the entire body is cold, the appropriate remedy is Dāng Guī Sì Nì Tāng. If there is agitation and counterflow, it should not be treated. According to “Bǎi Wèn,” cold Jue initially presents with coldness in the limbs, a submerged and thin pulse, and restless legs while lying down, possibly accompanied by aversion to cold, bluish nails, self-covering with clothing, or clear diarrhea, or self-regulating clear stools, frequent urination, and external symptoms of clarity; this is cold Jue, for which Sì Nì Tāng or Sān Wèi Shēn Yú Tāng can be chosen. Generally, conditions with more heat and less Jue are easier to treat, while more Jue and less heat are harder to remedy. There is also blood Jue, treated with Sì Wù Tāng; qi Jue with Sì Jūn Zǐ Tāng; summer Jue with Bái Hǔ Tāng; water Jue presents as palpitations below the heart, treated with Fú Líng Guì Gān Tāng; corpse Jue occurs due to toxin, caused by the offensive and improper atmosphere, and is treated with Mù Xiāng Yūn Qì Sǎn combined with Píng Wèi Sǎn or Zhuī Hūn Tāng. Worm Jue was mentioned earlier.[60p]
    Yang Jue

    Yang jue refers to the condition where, before the onset of a fit, there are symptoms such as headache, body heat, profuse sweating, constipation, thirst, and delirium. When the pathogen deeply infiltrates, it hides within and then leads to the fit. After a slight fit, within half a day, there is a recurrence of heat. If the heat descends, it results in abdominal pain, diarrhea with a feeling of heaviness, producing thick, sticky yellowish-red stools, likely containing pus and blood. If there is no blood in the stools, but heat rises, it will lead to a sore throat. Additionally, if the heat-induced fit persists for seven days without relief, there will inevitably be pus and blood in the stools. In the case of Yin jue, the symptoms include abdominal pain, diarrhea with clear stools, and not feeling thirsty; this reflects the specific nature of Yin jue, so one should refer back to the discussion on Yang jue.

    External Invasion of Cold and Heat

    Cold leading to blood and subsequent fits, with a pulse that is deep and weak, should be treated with Li Zhong Decoction or Si Ni Decoction. Heat that exhausts qi leading to fits, with a weak pulse, should be treated with Bai Hu Decoction or a modified Xiao Feng San with Qiang Huo. In summer, if labor commences which disturbs Yang Qi, leading to symptoms such as blindness and deafness, the Inner Canon refers to this as "boiling fit," indicating the heat forces damaging the kidneys and bladder. The appropriate treatment is Si Jun Zi Decoction, plus Yuan Zhi, Fang Feng, Chi Shao, Mai Men Dong, and Chen Pi. For any external causes leading to fits, it is advisable to add ginger juice to the dispersing medicines.[61p]

    Internal Injuries from the Seven Emotions

    Internal causes of injuries from joy and anger harm the qi and impair aspiration. When qi reverses and does not flow downwards, blood accumulates in the heart and chest. The "Inner Classic" refers to this as “slight collapse,” indicating an imbalance of yin and yang leading to turbulent qi and blood. In cases of severe cold from reversed qi, "Suhexiang Pills" and "Eight Flavor Releasing Qi Powder" can be applied. If anger causes intense reversal, resulting in vomiting blood or nosebleeds leading to collapse, modifications to "Four Substances Decoction" are made by removing Rehmannia and adding Red Poria, Ginseng, Platycodon, Aged Tangerine Peel, Ophiopogon, and Areca Nut, to be taken with ginger decoction, or "Six Depression Decoction."

    For abundant anger causing sudden shouting and collapse, phlegm is blocking upwards while fire rises from below, hence the use of five qian of Cyperus, seven qian of Chuanxiong, and three qian of fresh Licorice, boiled in the urine of children with ginger juice. Additionally, Qingdai (Indigo) and Human Zhongbai along with Cyperus made into pills may provide some relief, combined with "Phlegm Releasing Decoction" plus Huanglian and Cyperus, or swallowing "Angelica Dragon and Honey Pills."

    Injuries from labor, drinking, or being startled causing collapse can be treated with "Six Gentlemen Decoction" adding Qin, Lian, Bamboo Juice, and ginger juice.

    For internal injuries from phlegm-fire leading to collapse, a wiry and slippery pulse indicates the use of "Erchen Decoction" with Bamboo Juice. If there’s a cold condition, add fresh Aconite; if there’s a fiery condition, add Qin, Lian, Mountain Gardenia, and Bamboo Juice; for overweight individuals, add Ginseng and ginger juice.

    All forms of collapse can result in dizziness, fainting spells, hallucinations, or abdominal distension with difficulty in urination and defecation, or vomiting or heart pain, all indicative of phlegm-fire obstructing qi.[62p]
    Zang Jue

    Fever lasting seven to eight days, pulse thin, skin cold and restlessness, with vomiting or diarrhea, and no brief relief at any time—this is the true Qi of the Zang Yin exhausted, hence called Zang Jue. Zhongjing has no treatment, only the use of Si Ni Tang and cold drinks to rescue. In cases of Shao Yin Jue with vomiting, diarrhea, and restlessness, this is also not treated, but rescued with San Wei Shen Yu Tang.

    Caused by Alcohol and Lust

    Heat Jue occurs from excess drinking and indulgence, resulting in dampness stagnating in the Spleen Earth, unable to nourish the four limbs. Yang Qi is alone strong, thus causing palms and soles to feel hot. For this, use Bu Zhong Yi Qi Tang, Sheng Yang San Huo Tang, or Huo Yu Tang.

    Cold Jue occurs from excessive desires draining essence, severely damaging the Yuan Yang, unable to nourish the meridians, with Yin Qi predominating, hence all limbs feel cold. For this, use Shi Quan Da Bu Tang with Fu Zi, or Dang Gui Si Ni Tang.

    When ordinary Qi deficiency causes Jue, use Si Jun Zi Tang; for blood deficiency Jue, use Si Wu Tang. If there is heat, add Zhi Mu or Huang Bai; if there is deficiency-cold, add Fu Zi. However, cold Jue often arises from being overpowered. For example, if Kidney cold invades the Spleen, it leads to cold Jue; if Heart heat invades the Kidney, it leads to heat Jue—this applies across the Six Meridians.

    In acute discussions, Yang signs include thirst, delirium, and body heat; Yin signs show no thirst, lethargy, and coolness. This is similar to Yang Jue and Yin Jue in febrile diseases. Yet, mixed diseases often originate from alcohol, lust, and emotional disturbances, with few from external pathogens. Thus it is said that when Yang declines above, it causes cold Jue; when Yin declines below, it causes heat Jue. Yang extremes resemble Yin, and Yin extremes resemble Yang. Although the causes of febrile diseases differ, the variations in symptoms are also similar.[63p]
    Corpse Stiffness

    When one encounters a case of hanging or a disease related to death, or enters a temple and ascends a tomb, if suddenly afflicted by external evil influences, and faces opposition from internal toxicities, the qi becomes obstructed and fails to circulate; the meridians and pulse are suppressed, leading to confusion and loss of awareness. Suddenly, the hands and feet become cold, the face turns dark blue, the jaw tightens, and one may faint abruptly, possibly speaking nonsensically. It is critical not to treat this as a form of wind illness. First, administer the Suhexiang pill to awaken the patient; once they regain consciousness, use a combination of mukxiang and Pingwei powder to harmonize and adjust the qi.

    For those who are fundamentally weak, use five qian of sulfur and two qian of nitre, ground together into powder, and administer three doses prepared with a cup of aged wine, ensuring the sulfur is activated before pouring it into the cup to be taken warm, similar to a person walking five li and then taking another dose. If the previous medicines are unavailable, use the Ginseng and Aconite Decoction mixed with ginger juice and wine instead. Externally, perform moxibustion on the Baihui point with moxa the size of a mung bean for nine applications, and on the Qihai point for a hundred applications; if the body is warm, there's a chance for recovery.

    For those who died suddenly, administer the Chasing Soul Decoction.

    Treatment principles:

    When the yin and yang qi do not connect, it results in stiffness. For heat-induced stiffness, tonify the yin; for cold-induced stiffness, tonify the yang. The standard meridians indicate that supporting the dominant water calms the yang light, while enriching the source of fire dissipates the shadow of yin.

    In cases of sudden stiffness that have yet to be diagnosed, first administer the Suhexiang pill to awaken the patient. If there is phlegm blocking the mouth, use Guadi powder to induce vomiting, or it may be expelled through the nose as well. In cases of severe heat, apply the Dachengqi Decoction or the Shuangjiesan to purge.

    Medical Classics Must Reads 【Ming, Li Zhongzi】[64p]
    Damp Injury

    Those suffering from damp injury are affected by dampness, feeling heavy and painful in the body, with spontaneous sweating, slight fever, coldness in the lower legs, heaviness in the limbs, and a sense of fullness and discomfort in the chest and abdomen.

    Yin Syndrome

    In yang deficiency, the body is quiet, breathing is short, vision is blurred, breathing through the nose is difficult, difficulty in inhaling, fluids are not accepted, inability to control urination, the face appears pale and blackened as if scraped, or shows a preference for lying against a wall with closed eyes, not wanting to see anyone. The breath from the nose is cold, lips are not red, may appear white, blue, or purple, hands and feet are cold, nails are bluish or purplish, urine is white or light yellow, stools are loose, and upon pressing the stomach, there is heaviness without significant heat. If there is excessive yin, the cold may penetrate to the hands.

    Yin Toxicity

    Yin toxicity arises from a deficiency of the kidney and coldness, possibly from consuming cold substances, exposure to cold evil, or from sweating, vomiting, or purging, developing into yin toxicity. Symptoms include headache, abdominal cramping pain, eye pain, fatigue without significant heat, coldness in the limbs, cold sweat on the forehead and backs of the hands, diffuse pain in the body, unceasing spontaneous sweating, retching sounds, weak and shallow pulse, or a weak pulse at the inch and strong at the cubit. It is treatable within five days, but after six or seven days, it becomes difficult to treat. If cold damage progresses to the three yin, expect coldness in the limbs, abdominal pain and vomiting, cold saliva, fear of cold with shivering, a face appearing as if scraped, curled up clothing, a slow and soft pulse, and immediate warming is necessary. For lighter cases, use the "Regulating Middle Decoction"; for heavier cases, use the "Four Reverse Decoction." If the initial sickness does not present a fever but shows cold symptoms, it is termed a direct involvement of the yin channels and is similarly treated with these two decoctions.[65p]
    Similar Symptoms of Yin and Yang

    Symptoms that resemble Yang but are actually Yin include irritability, flushed face, feverish body, sore throat and thirst, a floating and faint pulse, cold hands and feet, diarrhea, clear urination, and excessive drowsiness. Additionally, there may be a feverish feeling accompanied by a desire for clothing, no thirst, and darkening nails, indicating an internal excess of Yin with true Yang lost.

    If there is syncope, sweating is not allowed.
    For syncope with reverse symptoms, vomiting is not permitted.
    In cases of reverse pulse that feels deep and thin, curled up with aversion to cold, covering oneself with clothes, not drinking water, and resulting in clear diarrhea, use Si Ni Tang. If the pulse is absent, use Tong Mai Si Ni Tang. If the pulse is slow and weak, use Li Zhong Tang. When hands and feet are slightly cold, it is termed "clear," and Li Zhong Tang should be used. For syncope with heat, employ Huangqi Ren Shen Jian Zhong Tang. If there is thirst with syncope, use Bai Hu Tang. If there is palpitations, first treat the condition using Fuling Gan Cao Tang. If there is syncope with heat and sleeplessness accompanied by delirium, Bai Hu Tang is indicated.

    When Yang is obstructed by evil in the chest, the Yang Qi fails to manifest, causing cold and reversal in the hands and feet, and the pulse becomes tense intermittently, leading to fullness and irritability in the heart, indicating a disease located in the chest. Zhongjing used Guadi San to induce vomiting.

    If there is initial fever followed by syncope with symptoms such as raised hands and thrown feet, irritability, aversion to heat when drinking, constipation, and dark urination, it indicates deep heat and serious syncope, with a deep and slippery pulse, sweating on the head and face, and warm nails, all suggesting lurking heat, treated with Da Xia Cheng Qi Tang.

    Severe Yin with vomiting and irritability leads to death from reverse symptoms.[66p]
    When experiencing cold limbs and diarrhea, one may be unable to eat; if they suddenly regain the ability to eat, this is called "removing the center," and it leads to death. In lesser yin disease, if one experiences cold limbs, reverse pulse, and has no pulse, and is treated with Bai Tong pig bile soup, if the pulse suddenly reappears, it indicates death. Excessive drinking and eating, or exposure to wind-cold, or being provoked by anger can lead to a blockage in the chest, preventing stomach qi from moving properly, resulting in sudden cold limbs, confusion, inability to speak, and inability to lift limbs. If mistakenly treated for stroke, this will certainly lead to death. It is advisable to prepare a ginger and salt soup to induce vomiting; for wind-cold symptoms, use Huo Xiang Zheng Qi powder, and for qi stagnation, use Ba Wei Shun Qi powder. After vomiting, if there are no other symptoms, simply adjust with herbs like Cang Zhu, Bai Zhu, Chen Pi, Hou Po, and Gan Cao.

    Guidelines for Diagnosis and Treatment by Wang Kentang, Ming Dynasty

    General Overview[67p]
    Luo Quanshan said: The Grand Guardian Wang stated that "jue" refers to a reversal of qi. In modern times, it is mistakenly referred to as "foot disease," but upon reading this, one realizes that in ancient times, it was called foot disease. The texts say: In cold jue, the hands and feet are cold; in hot jue, the hands and feet are hot. In wei jue, there is a combination of atrophy and jue, leading to weak and powerless feet. In bi jue, there is a combination of bi illness and jue illness, resulting in stubborn numbness, swelling, and pain in the feet. Qi rebelling can be seen in prior cases of cold jue, hot jue, wei jue, bi jue, and wind jue, leading to symptoms such as vomiting, confusion, bloating, shortness of breath, numbness in the lower abdomen, or sudden loss of consciousness, which the world refers to as foot disease attacking the heart. Nowadays, people also consider sudden fainting and loss of consciousness as jue. When asked how to interpret cold hands and feet as jue, it is said, "It is not." In Zhang Zhongjing's discussion of typhoid fever, a hot jue is indicated by hot hands and feet, and a cold jue by cold hands and feet, with cold being referred to as reversing. It is stated that any jue occurs when yin and yang do not harmoniously connect. Jue is characterized by the hands and feet being reversely cold. Therefore, in cases of yang deficiency that do not connect, one warms it; in cases where yang is trapped and deeply hidden, not harmonizing with yin, one purges it; where pathogenic heat invades but has not deeply penetrated, one disperses the transmitted yin heat, adjusting treatment according to the depth and severity. This pertains to the pathogenic factor of typhoid fever entering the interior, where the rebellious jue of the great yin hands and feet corresponds only to the two channels. Since it is a discussion of the pathogenic element transmitted through the six channels of typhoid, it is known that jue yin is the channel where the two yins intersect. When heat transmits to this location, it has reached an extreme depth, which is why it is called jue yin.[68p]
    The discourse in the "Inner Canon" on this topic is different. It states that the three yang channels originate from the surface of the five toes, while the three yin channels emerge from the inner sides of the same toes. Therefore, when the yang energy is abundant, the lower body becomes warm; when the yin energy prevails, cold rises from the toes to the knees. Regarding the cause of cold syndromes, it is said that the "front yin" is where the tendons gather, and it unites the taiyin and yangming channels. This occurs when a person is robust, during the autumn and winter when yin energy is strong and yang energy weakens. This results in an imbalance; the energy is depleted, and the flowing upwards is resisted, leading to an overflow of essence downward. Pathogenic energies ascend as a consequence, and the waning yang cannot nourish the meridians. As a result, yang diminishes each day while yin remains dominant, causing the hands and feet to feel cold.[69p]
    In discussing the causes of heat stroke, it is said that a person must often become drunk. If one indulges while full, the qi accumulates in the spleen, the kidney qi gradually declines, and the yang qi predominates, resulting in heat in the hands and feet. Observing its subtle essence, it likely relates to the kidneys receiving the essence of prenatal vitality, from which the original qi is filled, resulting in the interdependence of the two organs and the flow of the vessels. The operation of yin and yang within and without occurs, each with its own duties; yang governs the warming of the exterior, while yin governs the cooling of the interior. The vessels connect and circulate through the limbs; hence, I cite the example of the spleen and stomach being harmed by alcohol and lust, leading to severe cases of yin and yang disorders at the beginning of the text. Continuing to discuss the twelve channels of reversal, one only addresses the reversal of qi in the primary channels without mentioning the heat in the hands and feet or the reasons for the sickness; these cannot be fully explained in a single statement. Having established the two examples above, one can extend the inference to these matters, therefore it is not necessary to repeat them. Nevertheless, I will provide detailed exposition on other sections related to syncope to clarify the matter.[70p]
    According to the discussion on the Taiyin and Yangming: The Yangming involves the five organs and six bowels drawing vital energy from the stomach. Now, if the spleen is diseased and cannot assist the stomach in moving its fluids to the three Yin, the stomach likewise cannot transport fluids to the three Yang. Consequently, the limbs fail to receive the vital energy from food and grain, leading to progressive decline. The menstrual flow becomes unbeneficial, and the tendons, bones, and muscles lack the energy to thrive. In this context, the spleen and stomach oscillate between fullness and emptiness, with one being deficient and unable to move fluids while the other is excessive and acts independently. This independent action inevitably causes cold, which is one cause of syncope from the spleen and stomach. Furthermore, all twelve meridians draw vital energy from the stomach. If the stomach is cold, the energy in the meridians becomes cold; if the stomach is hot, the energy in the meridians becomes hot. Thus, when the meridians are not harmonized, there will inevitably be residual cold or heat at the extremities. This is the second cause of syncope from the spleen and stomach.[71p]
    Furthermore, the spleen and stomach are the organs that govern the kidneys. When the spleen and stomach are afflicted, they will impact the kidneys, where the kidneys regulate the lower body, especially in cases of syncope. If the kidneys are affected by pathogens, syncope occurs; this is the third cause of syncope related to the spleen and stomach. The "Lingshu" states: the Chong vessel, which shares its origin with the Shaoyin and the Great Luo from the kidneys, is the sea of the five organs and six bowels, thus all five organs and six bowels are nourished by it. The upper part of the Chong vessel nourishes Yang, and infuses essence; the lower part connects to the Shaoyin channel, moistens the three Yin, infuses the collaterals, and warms the muscles. If the collateral networks are blocked, the diaphragm becomes immobile; if it is immobile, syncope results, leading to coldness. The Chong vessel is responsible for the Yang that governs the internal functions of the kidneys; Yang is associated with fire. If this Yang moves properly, it is a minor fire that nourishes life; if it moves chaotically, it becomes a strong fire that harms, and excessive fire weakens the original true Yang. Dongyuan stated that fire and original Qi cannot coexist; when one prevails, the other is diminished. As previously discussed regarding syncope, when the Qi is downward and conflicts arise upwards, the pathogenic Qi follows, not from a separate source, but from the chaos of this fire, causing the tumultuous energies of Yin and Yang to rise in rebellion.[72p]
    Therefore, it is said that the disease of the Chong Vessel arises from reversed qi, and the pathogenic mechanisms indicate that all reversed influences ascending are associated with fire, while all collapses are associated with the lower part. However, when the yin and yang of the kidneys are harmonized, treatments for the lower part are effective; if they are not harmonized, treatments for the lower part will fail, leading to the emergence of heat and cold collapses. This is the pathway of the kidneys concerning collapses.

    As for the Chong Vessel and the stomach, both are named after the sea; hence, the diseases of collapse in the twelve vessels are all related. Additionally, other texts discussing collapses mention that excess yang qi can lead to an outburst, where essence is exhausted and accumulations form in summer, causing people to experience collapses. There are also conditions where two yangs and one yin cause wind collapse; cases where lack of determination leads to collapses; organ collapses; sudden and unrecognized collapses; and collapses where all five collaterals are exhausted.

    All these causes, arising from reversed evils, involve the intertwining of fire and the five types of fire, leading to disruptions in the qi of the vessels, thus resulting from the interactions of the five qi: wind, cold, dryness, dampness, and heat.[73p]
    The five energies often transform and interact with one another. Wind and dryness transform together into heat; dampness and cold transform together into cold symptoms. When illness is severe, the limbs may be influenced by the cold and heat into a state of reversal. The rebellious qi that rises from below must rely on the fiery strength to achieve this. The San Jiao, foot Shao Yang, Chong, Ren, and Du meridians all govern the fire below, yet they all belong to the kidneys. Why is this? The foot Shao Yin and hand Shao Yin interact at midday, residing in between; this allows the kidney's original qi to be stabilized. If the ministerial fire is improperly activated, one must first support the kidneys and regulate the treatment to redirect the rebellious qi, differentiating the strengths of the five energies to adjust them appropriately. However, there are also cases where the body does not become cold due to the uprising of qi. As stated in "The Original Patterns of Disease," yin water governs purity, thus if there is cold illness, the four limbs may feel cold. This indicates that yang is weak while yin is present, resulting in cold. This corresponds to the principle stated by Zhongjing that one experiences reversal before heat; due to the cold pathogen from the outside, the yang qi is unable to overcome it, hence the initial state of reversal. Though evils may be categorized as internal and external, in the context of warming, they are essentially singular.[74p]
    Blood Collapse

    There are individuals who, without any previous illness, suddenly become lifeless, their bodies immobile and silent, unaware of others, with closed eyes that cannot open, and mouths that cannot speak. They may have a slight awareness of others but dislike hearing any sounds, feeling as though in a daze, only regaining consciousness after a while. This condition is caused by excessive sweating, leading to a deficiency of blood and qi being stagnant in the blood, with yang rising but not descending. Qi becomes blocked and fails to circulate, resulting in a death-like state. When qi returns to the blood and yin and yang are restored, consciousness returns after some time. This is called "stagnation collapse," also known as "blood collapse." It is often found in women. The treatment should be Baiwei Decoction or Cangong Powder.

    Various Types of Collapse

    Collapse is not a fixed illness. Yang collapse supplements yin, nourishing the master of water; yin collapse supplements yang, enhancing the source of fire. These forms of yin and yang collapse differ from the yin and yang collapses associated with cold damage. In cases of cold damage, yang collapse requires the method of pushing out the old to bring in the new, while yin collapse requires the use of Fuzi to regulate the center. The differences are as pronounced as between ice and fire, with life and death within reach.

    When yang qi is weak and deficient, yin must compensate, causing cold to travel from the fingers to the knees, known as cold collapse. This is an inversion of cold downward, treated with Liuwu Fuzi Decoction. When yin retreats, yang advances; therefore, when yin qi weakens below, yang moves to replenish it, resulting in warmth in the feet. When the heat intensifies, it may ascend along the three yin pathways, termed heat collapse, which should be treated with Liuw Wei Di Huang Pill. The liver stores blood and governs anger; thus, when anger arises, fire ascends in the liver, carrying blood upward, resulting in chaotic blood and qi within the chest, leading to a reversal and termed thin collapse, which should be treated with Puhuang Decoction.[75p]
    All movements belong to Yang; thus, when one is overworked, it disturbs the Yang, causing the Yang energy to expand. When the Yang energy expands, the fire of labor becomes excessive. Excessive fire causes water to dry up, leading to exhaustion of essence. This results in prolonged accumulation, and by summer, both internal and external heat increase, further depleting water and increasing fire. The solitary Yang becomes reversed, as if simmering or boiling; thus, it is called "simmering reversal," for which Ren Shen Gu Ben Wan is recommended.

    The Qi of the five corpses, through excessive indulgence, disrupts the balance of Yin and Yang, affecting Qi and blood. Above there is the obstruction of absolute Yang, and below there is the severing of Yin connections, causing disconnection of form and Qi. This leads to a violent reversal resembling death, named "corpse reversal," for which Er Shi Si Wei Liu Qi Yin and Su He Xiang Wan are recommended.

    Cold phlegm leads to confusion, with the extremities feeling cold; this is termed "phlegm reversal," for which Jiang Fu Tang is recommended. Stomach cold may cause the expulsion of roundworms, known as "roundworm reversal," for which Wu Mei San with Li Zhong Tang is recommended.

    Qi is the Yang of the human body; any stagnation leads to the inability of Yang Qi to circulate, resulting in coldness of the hands and feet, resembling a stroke. However, in a stroke, the body tends to be warm, and in Qi reversal, the body is cold. This is termed "Qi reversal," for which Ba Wei Shun Qi San is recommended.[76p]
    Discussion on the Essentials of Classics

    In the section detailing pulse diagnosis, it is stated that "厥" refers to the internal depletion, meaning the loss of the essence and vital energy of the five internal organs. "喑" indicates an inability to produce sound. "俳" refers to the body's limbs being impaired. Nowadays, when people encounter these symptoms, they will likely label them as stroke without realizing it results from internal depletion and kidney deficiency. This is because sound originates from the lungs and fundamentally relies on the kidneys, while physical strength is rooted in blood and fundamentally depends on essence. The foundation of vital energy primarily lies in the kidneys; thus, when the lesser yin is insufficient, it results in "厥." Furthermore, the essay on regulating the meridians states that a deficiency of will leads to "厥." The section on the spirit indicates that a deficiency in kidney energy leads to "厥." Observing these discussions, it is clear that the essence of "厥" is not related to wind. Therefore, for treating this condition, one should emphasize methods that are not based on wind symptoms. The essay on regulating the meridians indicates that both energy and blood can be deficient, revealing an imbalance of yin and yang. If both energy and blood travel upwards, it indicates a deficiency of yin below and a lack of root in the spirit, resulting in the separation of yin and yang, which may cause sudden death. If the condition is slight, recovery can be achieved; if it is severe, the situation is critical.[77p]
    This is precisely what people today refer to as an abrupt fall or collapse due to a stroke, which is also a stroke caused by phlegm-heat obstructing the upper body, while failing to realize that it actually stems from a deficiency in the lower body. However, the condition of the upper body being 'full' is only a false fullness, and in severe cases, it should be slightly cleared. The deficiency in the lower body is a true emptiness; if there are no pathogenic factors to address, then one must promptly strengthen the lower body decisively.

    In observing the passages of the Yangming classic regarding the linking of channels and organs, it is stated that 'reversal of qi' is significant, which is quite evident and straightforward! The linking of channels indicates that the illness lies in the muscles, hence it is light and produces symptoms; whereas, the linking of organs indicates that the illness lies at the root, thus it is heavy and fatal. Having recognized this as a reversal of qi, it can be discerned that symptoms like abrupt falls and collapses are not truly strokes, but various scholars in Hejian refer to the central organs, which leads to confusion between true and false strokes, making it difficult to distinguish.

    The detailed discussions in the Great Righteousness Treatise and the Subtle Analysis Treatise state: "When the pulse reaches a state resembling panting, it indicates a pulse that is rapid and urgent like breathlessness, reflecting a condition of chaotic blood and qi, hence resulting in sudden reversal and inability to speak," which aligns with what people today refer to as a 'silent stroke.'[78p]
    In Yunyang, the upper part is hot and the lower part is cold; this indicates a condition where water and fire do not interact, leading to a state of blindness, akin to dizziness caused by wind. Those who cannot speak are suffering issues related to the lungs and kidneys, while those who are dizzy have problems linked to the liver and spleen. For those who temporarily regain consciousness, recovery can occur when the Qi returns; however, if the Yin deteriorates, it is a very dangerous condition. All should be treated according to the previous principles to address the root cause.

    In the discussion of the "End and Beginning" chapter, it states: sudden clarity refers to sudden coldness; if it feels like being split or severed, it is described as painful and indescribable, which aligns with the so-called confusion and disturbance caused by wind. Those with heat often experience warmth and a strong pulse, thus it is advisable to clear the fire within the Yin. Those with phlegm often suffer from wheezing and have a slippery pulse, thus it is necessary to relieve the phlegm in the upper jiao. For those without fire or phlegm, they tend to feel cold and have a weak pulse, hence it is necessary to tonify the primordial Qi. In cases with such symptoms coupled with confusion and loss of consciousness, this represents a severe condition involving multiple organs, often difficult to treat.

    The "Connecting Qi to the Heavens" chapter explains that convulsions due to heat are a type of heat stroke, caused by overexertion accumulating during summer, which can now be referred to as heat wind.[79p]
    If one sees a person suddenly collapse with convulsions and phlegm, they should immediately treat them using methods for heat stroke. "Thin prickle" refers to a situation of acute pressure caused by great anger, leading to conditions such as qi and blood stasis; the treatment approach should be as follows.

    According to the detailed analysis of deficiencies and excesses, diseases caused by rich foods and liquors harm the spleen and kidneys; diseases arising from sudden worries are related to the sadness that harms the lungs; internal qi being violently depleted relates to anger affecting the liver. All these are considered internal injuries. If there is an external wind causing harm rather than internal issues, it will certainly affect the meridians, leading to symptoms of emaciation and painful obstruction. Thus, the distinction between internal injuries and external attacks can be understood and should not be confused.

    In detail regarding the principles of reversal and order about fat and thin, the pathways run from the head down to the feet, from the back to the abdomen. All organs and meridians, including the five zang organs, six fu organs, and twelve meridians, are interconnected; thus, they are referred to as the sea of the five zang, six fu, and twelve meridians. The sea is the source of all rivers; hence, wherever the meridians originate, there is a sense of convergence. This understanding applies to the ebb and flow of blood and qi throughout the body.[80p]
    The Chong Vessel originates in the womb, which is where its meridians converge and where vital energies accumulate. Hence, it is referred to as the Sea of Blood or the Gate of Life; this is what is called the fundamental abode. If one indulges in passions, leading to the depletion of the source of essence and energy, conditions such as syncope and sudden collapse will arise from this. Otherwise, how is it that one can suddenly fall, with both spirit and body in decline, utterly oblivious to surroundings? When it reaches this point, is it merely a disease of a single meridian or organ? Upon examination, it becomes clear that it is indeed due to the collapse of the Chong Vessel, without a doubt. Therefore, anyone who treats this by seeking relief without addressing the root cause, it is certain that such reasoning is flawed! Specifically regarding the discussion of the condition of shock from external evil influence, it is associated with sudden onset of malign conditions. This includes all forms of improper energies across the four seasons, as well as mountain spirits and soil demons, along with all sorts of phantoms and nightmares.[81p]
    When someone suffers from this condition, their hands and feet suddenly become cold, their skin shivers, their complexion turns dark and pale, and their spirit is unsettled. They may speak nonsensically or be unable to speak, with phlegm obstructing their throat. They might also experience dizziness and lose consciousness; this is referred to as "corpse syncope" or "sudden syncope." Acupuncture should be employed, as detailed in the classic texts. If moxibustion is used, the methods of Qin Chengzu and Hua Tuo for moxibustion on the yang are particularly effective.

    In all medicinal practices, one must understand that the presence of evils is due to qi deficiency; therefore, in the classic texts, it is mentioned to use blood from the left side to replenish the Five Meridians that are depleted, which is clear in meaning. In such dangerous moments, without employing herbs like ginseng and Aconite to restore yang, how can one recover? If the evil qi is overwhelming and the chest is obstructed, one must first clear the passages before regulating. One should primarily use formulas like Jin Zheng Qi San, Liu Qi Yin, green onion and ginger soup, or Su He Wan.

    A discussion on the strength and weakness of formulas concludes that counteracting the path of qi is the same as syncope. The interplay of yin and yang qi is such that yang rises from the left and yin descends from the right; therefore, for yang illnesses, the left side is more severe, and for yin illnesses, the right side is more severe. When the rising does not rise and the descending does not descend, it is the reversal of the natural flow of qi.[82p]
    The vitality of a person must ascend from below; therefore, the energy of the elderly has declined from below, and what arises from above is in accordance with nature. In contrast, the vitality of youth is initially strong from below, and what rises from below is in accordance with nature. If an old person’s spirit declines from above, the energy at their end is predictable; when the young person’s body weakens from below, the energy at their beginning is predictable as well, both are cases of reversal. When one becomes ill and cannot rise or fall, it indicates that their foundation has deteriorated, hence the cold and extremities reach to the knees. The youth, with their yang energy still vigorous, face decomposition during autumn and winter; the elderly, with their yang energy inherently diminished and more sensitive to cold, therefore have no worries in autumn and winter. All these diseases of inversion, where yang appears not to be yang and yin seems not to be yin, create a separation of the five organs, leaving no evidence to verify. If one cannot complete their days, it signifies their serious decline. Furthermore, in dreams where one is utterly confused and disoriented, this results from the heart and kidneys not communicating, causing the spirit to scatter and stray, thus leading to inversion. Such conditions are fundamentally linked to the roots of the lesser yin.[83p]
    The discussion of cold and hot collapse (jue) involves the hands and feet as terms, but essentially speaks to the disorder of yin and yang. Cold collapse represents a loss due to usage, while hot collapse arises from excessive drinking. This is because the energies of yin and yang invariably manifest in the hands and feet; thus, when collapse is about to occur, cold and heat numbness must first emerge from the hands and feet. When it becomes severe, it may manifest in various ways, potentially affecting the five organs and six bowels, each presenting its own symptoms. Therefore, the collapse of the hands and feet serves primarily as a visible sign, and by recognizing slight indications, one should consider the underlying causes.

    Examining Zhang Zhongjing's views on reverse collapse, there are notable differences from the "Inner Canon." In the "Inner Canon," discussions concerning the hands and feet distinguish between cold and hot collapse, whereas Zhang Zhongjing focuses solely on the aspect of cold reversal as a form of collapse. Furthermore, concerning the discussion of evil and righteous energies, the "Inner Canon" addresses the original qi, hence the manifold symptoms, encompassing both qi and blood, which all bear critical signs; in contrast, Zhang Zhongjing emphasizes the role of evil qi, considering the hands and feet only, with fears directed towards the descent of yin and the retreat of yang. Cheng Wujide states: collapse signifies the predominance of yin, which is understandable.[84p]

    Argument

    The signs of reversal (or counterflow) are those of danger. The term 'reversal' signifies a complete disruption or chaos, representing the disorder of qi and blood. This is why the "Inner Canon" places special emphasis on it and explains it in detail. It states that sudden and violent reversals are both general names for this condition. For instance, terms like cold reversal and hot reversal differentiate the yin and yang aspects of the condition. When mentioning connected channels and organs, it discusses the life and death aspects of reversal. Moreover, the distinctions among the various channels and organs have also been elaborated upon.

    Additionally, in recent times, there are still signs such as qi reversal, blood reversal, phlegm reversal, alcohol reversal, and ascaris reversal, all of which are fundamentally based on these principles. Observing the teachings of the "Inner Canon," they appear to be quite clear; however, later generations still fail to recognize this. Whenever they encounter these signs, they tend to label them all as a stroke, unaware of what reversal truly signifies, thus treating them as wind disorders, which can lead to greater harm. I am deeply saddened by this, which is why I have made efforts to clarify previous misinterpretations of wind conditions.

    As for the treatment of these conditions, it should be approached as treating the previous non-wind signs, using them in conjunction. This is precisely how one should address reversal. Any remaining similar signs will still be listed in subsequent sections.[85p]

    Discussion on Treatment

    In cases of cold extremities, the limbs must be cool, with a pulse that is deep, faint, and infrequent; or, even if frequent, it is weak. The patient may fear cold and prefer warmth, covering themselves with clothing, or may experience diarrhea of clear fluids, and the physical symptoms are generally weak. Although these cases all pertain to cold syndromes, there are many conditions that resemble heat but are not actually heat-related. Therefore, whenever there is cold extremity with pulse and symptoms not indicative of real heat, it is certainly cold extremity without doubt.

    Hot extremities must first show numerous heat-related symptoms, with a pulse that is deep, slippery, and rapid; the patient fears heat and prefers coolness, may wave their hands and kick their feet, or feel restless, with constipation and dark stools, and the physical symptoms are often marked by confusion or stupor.

    When treating these two conditions, one should apply methods distinct from treating cold and heat conditions associated with wind. As for the case of cold extremities resulting from cold damage, both its yin and yang aspects should be discerned using this method. However, in cases of cold extremities due to cold damage, the distinction lies in the pathogenic qi, hence cold extremities should be treated with warming methods, while hot extremities should be addressed with purging methods.[86p]
    In the "Neijing," the essential focus is on the primordial qi. Therefore, for heat syncope, one must supplement yin, and for cold syncope, one must supplement yang. The treatment of both cases cannot be overlooked!

    The symptoms of qi syncope are of two types, as both qi deficiency and qi excess can lead to syncope. In cases of sudden collapse due to qi deficiency, the body will appear weak, the color will be pale, the limbs will feel slightly cold, and the pulse will be weak—this indicates a loss of qi. One should use remedies such as ginseng, astragalus, angelica, poria, rehmannia, goji berries, or great rejuvenation decoctions; in severe cases, remedies like Huayan Decoction or Ginseng Decoction should be used.

    In cases of syncope due to qi excess, the body will appear agitated, the pulse will be deep, taut, and slippery, and there will be fullness in the chest and diaphragm—this indicates a reversal of qi. In cases of great anger, the body and qi may collapse, leading to blood pooling above; this is related. Treatment should include dispersing Qi Decoction, Four Molar Decoction, Eight Flavors Tranquilizing Powder, or Su He Xiang Pill to first normalize the qi, then subsequently regulate according to the condition of deficiency or excess.[87p]
    If one becomes angry and harms the qi, causing it to be reversed and thus impairing the true qi, it indicates that the qi was initially weak. If one is prone to much anxiety and fear, leading to a timid and trapped qi, this weakness becomes even more apparent. If one uses methods meant to regulate the qi and resolve stagnation in such cases, it would be a mistake.

    There are two types of blood stasis: one caused by blood loss and the other by blood reversal, both of which can lead to stasis. Blood loss occurs in cases of severe bleeding or excessive blood loss during childbirth, leading to a loss of qi as well, hence causing sudden collapse and death. In such cases, one should first pinch the philtrum or burn vinegar with charcoal to gather the qi, and urgently administer one or two taels of ginseng decoction to restore the qi, ensuring it does not completely escape; this will lead to gradual recovery. Then, according to the conditions of cold or heat, one may proceed to regulate it accordingly; this is called replenishing the qi after blood loss.

    If one does not understand this and merely uses medicines for the blood, even a small amount of qi could suddenly dissipate, leaving the yin with nothing to support it, and thus there would be no vitality. If one considers using cold and cooling methods to stop the bleeding, it will certainly damage the yang qi, hastening death.[88p]
    Blood reversal refers to the phenomenon where blood and qi move together upwards, and it is also described as a condition where intense anger causes the manifestation of qi to be disrupted, leading to blood accumulating above. When blood is affected by reverse qi, it is essential to first regulate the qi; when qi flows smoothly, blood will follow suit. It is advisable to use formulas like Tongyu Decoction or Huagan Decoction to address this condition and, once blood circulation is restored and qi is relaxed, to treat according to the specific symptoms.

    When phlegm and fluids obstruct the body, causing qi to become stagnant and mental confusion, and neither medication nor food can alleviate it, one must prioritize either inducing vomiting or relieving the blockage to address the urgent condition. Once the phlegm and qi have opened slightly, one should then treat the root cause of the illness. If phlegm is caused by heat, it should be cleared and driven down; if caused by wind and cold, it should be dispersed and warmed; if caused by dampness, it should be dried and regulated; if caused by spleen deficiency, the spleen should be strengthened; if caused by kidney deficiency, the kidneys should be nourished. Treating the underlying causes will allow the phlegm to clear naturally. However, there are cases where phlegm cannot be treated directly, as pushing too hard may worsen the condition and exacerbate the phlegm.[89p]
    Various methods for treating phlegm can be found in the previous section regarding the treatment of phlegm not caused by wind. The symptoms of "alcoholic syncope" correspond to those described as "heat syncope" in the classics. Furthermore, the classic also mentions "alcohol wind," which belongs to this category. Those who indulge in drinking without restraint often suffer from this illness. When their vital energy is strong and can overcome it, they do not recognize the harm of alcohol; but when they begin to weaken, the effects of alcohol become frightening. There are many ailments caused by alcohol, and their origins are unknown. When it comes to alcoholic syncope, it closely resembles a stroke: in mild cases, the person may still be aware, but in severe cases, they may suddenly collapse, become unresponsive, or exhibit sudden confusion, agitation, inability to speak, excessive phlegm, breathlessness, fever, cough, or even spitting blood. If one observes that the stools are dry and the pulse is strong and prefers cold, this indicates excess heat and dampness obstructing upward, and it is advisable to use methods like extracting the fuel (removing the underlying cause) and drinking to swiftly cool the fire.[90p]
    In cases of extreme heat, one should alternate with drinks made from pear syrup and mung bean to counteract the poison. It is crucial to avoid items that are spicy and dry, to gradually reduce damp heat and restore some energy, and only then use nourishing yin remedies for subsequent care. If there is constipation or a weak pulse without signs of heat, one should not clear heat but instead use remedies like Erchen Decoction, Liu Junzi Decoction, or Jinshui Liujun Decoction.

    If due to alcohol, the yin is harmed leading to the dual deficiency of spleen and kidney, and this results in syncope, one must promptly address the root cause; otherwise, there will be no benefit. Anyone suffering from this should avoid alcohol for life, ensuring they do not let it touch their lips; ignorance of this will make relapse very difficult.

    There are two types of symptoms of color loss: one is sudden collapse, and the other is blood movement. The sudden collapse of color is typically due to the person being fundamentally deficient, occasionally exerting themselves to the fullest in unusual circumstances; or it may occur after a prolonged indulgence in love and desire. Therefore, after such incidents, Qi departs along with essence, resulting in a sudden collapse that cannot be reversed.[91p]
    One should urgently press the Ren Zhong point, and have the individual embrace tightly, facing each other, ensuring that warm breath circulates between them to connect their vital energy, and not allow the energy to escape, in order to preserve the spirit. Then, quickly administer Duhua Decoction or apply strong moxibustion to the Qihai point several times to restore the Yang energy, which might help revive them. However, due to the urgency of the situation, how can one be meticulous in treatment? Thus, it often results in failure to save them. This discussion is specifically about those who are in immediate distress, and indeed, such cases are not many. Yet, there are many who suffer from latent conditions. How do I know this? Because their essence diminishes over time, and their vital energy gradually depletes. Therefore, symptoms often appear two or three days after engaging in sexual activity. But since the problem does not manifest immediately, it is not viewed as an illness. Moreover, many people are reluctant to talk about this, unaware that after middle age, many fall ill because of it; this is what is referred to as "color and syncope." Unfortunately, the practitioners of the time cannot discern this and simply attribute it to strokes.[92p]
    For those treating this condition, one should primarily focus on nourishing and supporting the Mingmen (life gate), whether using water or fire, and the treatment should be based on methods for addressing the "non-wind gate."

    Moreover, the movement of blood characterized by color changes occurs because both blood and qi rush upward, which is also categorized as blood congestion. However, this is different from blood reversing due to intense anger, and thus, the treatment methods also vary. This condition often arises from the upward blazing of the desire fire, causing the blood to follow the qi upward. It typically involves strong emotional disturbances and the inability to express desires, and sometimes it involves using curative herbs to strongly suppress the excessive fire.

    Symptoms may include sudden and violent vomiting, nosebleeds that cannot be controlled, cold reversals, sweating, shortness of breath, or coughing—all of which result from the upward surge of yin fire.

    In treating this, one must first suppress the fire to control its momentum, clear the fullness, and transform excessive dampness, primarily using Four Yin Decoction or modified versions of similar prescriptions. If there is depletion of yin below and fire does not return to its source, with no other troubling heat pulse signs, and continuous blood congestion leading to a critical state, it is essential to use Zhen Yin Decoction, as it alone can provide rescue. Once the condition stabilizes, one can then adjust treatment according to the specific symptoms.[93p]
    Yin and Yang Syndromes

    There are two types of syndromes: Yang syndrome and Yin syndrome. Yang syndrome, or heat syndrome, occurs when heat travels from the three Yang channels into the Yin system. It initially manifests as headache and fever, starting shallow and progressing deeper, eventually affecting the three Yin channels, leading to coldness in the limbs and sometimes warmth. The symptoms include constipation, restlessness, delirium, thirst, aversion to heat rather than cold, and a deep, strong pulse. This is caused by the transformation of heat in the channels; externally, the hands and feet may feel cold, but internally it is due to a pathogenic heat. When Yang syndrome manifests, it resembles Yin, hence it is called Yang syndrome. The symptoms arise from internal heat or loss of Yang at its descending point. If the syndrome is mild, the heat is also mild, and remedies like Si Ni San are appropriate; if severe, then stronger remedies like Cheng Qi Decoction are needed.

    Yin syndrome, or cold syndrome, does not initially show signs of heat from the three Yang channels. Instead, true cold directly invades the three Yin channels, causing aversion to cold, abdominal pain, vomiting, diarrhea, shivering, and a lack of thirst, with a deep, weak pulse. This syndrome is characterized by the absence of Yang, thus it is called Yin syndrome. For mild cases, Li Zhong Decoction is suitable, while for severe cases, Si Ni Decoction or Hui Yang Decoction are recommended.

    Note that the differentiation between Yang and Yin syndromes is as described; this is a fundamental principle of the ancients. However, I humbly offer my distinctions.[94p]
    In cases of Yin inversion, where there are no Yang symptoms or Yang pulse but the illness manifests as cold, it is clearly a Yin condition. Nowadays, people simply call it middle cold, which is indeed the disease. However, few fall into this error, and it's not difficult to understand. The treatment should be to warm the center without needing further distinction.

    As for Yang inversion, distinguishing it is necessary. Inversion arises from the transmission of the three Yangs, leading to Yang inversion, which is indeed the case. Even in discussing transmission through the channels, can it be said that there are no Yin conditions? Therefore, any illness resulting from true Yang deficiency is also a Yin inversion within Yang; a weak pulse with no spirit is also a Yin inversion within Yang; excessive attack or cooling leads to a Yin inversion within Yang as well.

    The four limbs are the roots of all Yang; unless there are symptoms like heat accumulation, restlessness, thirst, or fullness, inverted coldness indicates a deficiency of Yang energy. Cheng Wujie stated: generally, inversion is related to Yin, and is often associated with coldness. He also said: inversion is the amplification of Yin. Therefore, conditions of combined deficiency and coldness, although they may arise from the Yang channels, are still a Yin inversion within Yang.

    Should Yin within Yin be warmed, or should Yin within Yang be cooled? Scholars should not think that the presence of headache and fever, merely due to the transmission of the three Yangs, automatically classifies it as Yang inversion, as treating cold caused by heat would lead to significant harm.[95p]
    Hui Jue (Intestinal Twists)

    Cheng Wujiy stated: "Zang Jue (Organ Collapse) leads to death, as the yang energy is severed. Although Hui Jue involves convulsions and distress, once the gut contents are expelled, the person will calm down. However, this is not preferable to Zang Jue, which is accompanied by restlessness, with no moment of peace. The patient suffers from cold organs and a weak stomach, thus it is advisable to administer Wumei Wan to warm the organs and stabilize the worms. Both Zang Jue and Hui Jue are related to febrile diseases, as detailed in the section on Febrile Diseases."[96p]
    Secret Record of the Stone Chamber
    By Qing Dynasty Chen Shiduo

    The treatment method for the condition involves understanding that when one experiences sudden coldness and pain in the limbs, it is often mistaken for a wind-related issue of the extremities. In reality, it is the internal heat of the heart that cannot be released, leading to cold limbs while the chest and abdomen feel hot. The prescription consists of three qian of Chai Hu, two qian of Dang Gui, one qian of Jing Jie, two qian of Huang Lian, two qian of stir-fried Zhi Zi, one qian of Ban Xia, and one qian of Zhi Ke, which should be decocted in water. One dose will stabilize the condition, while two doses will lead to complete recovery. This is because many cases of cold limbs are due to internal fire, and intense coldness corresponds to intense heat. Thus, by relieving internal heat, the limbs will warm up. The brilliance of this formula lies in using Chai Hu as the chief ingredient, which, along with other cooling herbs, directly enters the heart and liver without causing stagnation in the chest. Chai Hu is adept at dispersing external and internal evils, and is effective in alleviating pent-up energy. Merely treating the limbs without addressing the core issue would be like capturing the underlings without catching the leader; the remaining forces would remain uncontained. Therefore, the focus should not be solely on the limbs but primarily on the heart and chest.

    While many cases of this condition are indeed caused by internal heat, there are also instances of coldness that lead to the same symptoms, resulting from chilled Yin entering the kidney palace. This will inevitably challenge the heart's fire with the kidney's cold water being on the rise, leading to a weakened ruling fire while the subordinate becomes strong. If one does not urgently use strong heating remedies to save the heart, a crisis could ensue at any moment.[97p]
    To treat, use three qian of ginseng, one liang of white atractylodes, one qian each of aconite, cinnamon, and evodia. Boil with water and take as a decoction; one dose will cure. However, it is crucial to distinguish between cold and heat patterns, as they are vastly different. In cold qi deficiency, the hands and feet will be blue, drinking water will induce vomiting, and there will be abdominal pain, with a preference for warmth for relief. In contrast, with heat qi deficiency, the hands and feet may feel cold but will not appear bluish; water will not induce vomiting, and warmth will exacerbate abdominal pain. If one can accurately diagnose and appropriately use medicine, the treatment will be effective as soon as it reaches the throat—this is the mark of a true healer, and there is no need for talismans or incantations.

    For treatment, if it is a case of corpse pattern due to evil qi blockage, medication must be used to open it. Do not use the gourd stem powder to induce vomiting; instead, use soapberry to provoke sneezing. Use seven gourd stems, and prepare with two bowls of water, reducing it to one bowl of decoction, adding a little salt to administer, which will induce a heavy cough with thick phlegm, resulting in cure. Alternatively, grind soapberry spines into fine powder, fill a goose feather quill with the powder, and blow it into the patient's nose. If they sneeze and expel thick phlegm resembling yellow substances, they will be cured. Most cases of qi deficiency are due to heat evil, yet the heat evil must manifest in cries; now, silently and lifelessly, resembling a corpse, it is clear this is a case of yin deficiency suddenly struck by yin evil. Therefore, do not treat a yang deficiency with methods meant for it, as this could lead to failure. It is better to first use gourd stem and soapberry to induce vomiting to remove phlegm. Once the voice returns, treat with five qian of ginseng, one qian of white peony, three qian of poria, five qian of white atractylodes, and two qian of pinellia to restore stability.[98p]
    This is indeed a method for opening treatment. The method for inducing sneezing is to grind three qian of fresh Pinellia into a powder, form it into water balls the size of yellow beans, and insert them into the nostrils; it will surely induce persistent sneezing. Drink water, and it will stop immediately. This method treats conditions like wind stroke, speechlessness, and corpse-like stupor, as well as demonic possession; all are excellent methods for opening treatment.
